Foolish Hearts

When Claudia accidentally eavesdrops on the epic breakup of Paige and Iris, the it-couple at her school, she finds herself in hot water with prickly, difficult Iris. Thrown together against their will in the class production of A Midsummer Night's Dream, along with the goofiest, cutest boy Claudia has ever known, Iris and Claudia are in for an eye-opening senior year.

Smart, funny, and thoroughly, wonderfully flawed, Claudia navigates a world of intense friendships and tentative romance in Emma Mills's Follish Hearts, a young adult novel about expanding your horizons, allowing yourself to be vulnerable, and accepting‚Äîand loving‚Äîpeople for who they really are.

one

It‚Äôs at Amber Brunati‚Äôs annual Pink Party that everything begins to unravel.

The invitation—on thick pink paper, naturally, with gold and turquoise swirls—had declared it to be the last great luncheon of the summer. As if my summer had been packed to bursting with a whole host of other themed luncheons instead of babysitting jobs and shifts at Pinky‚Äôs Sub Shop. It also implied that there had been a number of mediocre luncheons this summer, as this was meant to be the last of the great ones.

I stare around Amber‚Äôs backyard at clustered tables covered in pink gingham cloths and at the girls around said tables. We‚Äôre all wearing pink except for Iris Huang, who had the nerve to arrive in lavender (Amber‚Äôs angry whispers carried clear across the lawn), and Kaitlyn Winthrop, who is technically wearing magenta. This seems to incense Amber even more, because while we all know that Iris‚Äôs dress is a big official eff you to the entire Pink Party construct, Kaitlyn doesn‚Äôt seem to realize that she‚Äôs committed a faux pas.

‚ÄúSomeone get that girl a color wheel,‚Äù Amber hisses angrily to Madison Lutz, sitting to my left. ‚ÄúSomeone get her a fucking Pantone booklet because magenta is not pink. We all know magenta is not pink, right?‚Äù She looks to me. ‚ÄúRight?‚Äù

‚ÄúAbso-tootin-lutely!‚Äù I declare loudly, because I am a moron.

It‚Äôs quiet for a split second, and then a laugh escapes from Madison.

Amber doesn‚Äôt laugh, but her lips twitch in amusement. ‚ÄúYes. Good. Thank you, Claudia. Glad we‚Äôre all on the same page.‚Äù

In truth, we are rarely all on the same page. More often than not, they‚Äôre all on one page, and I‚Äôm on a completely different one. It can‚Äôt be helped most of the time. Society itself puts us on different pages. They drive Range Rovers and have celebrity deejays at their sweet sixteens. I had to scrape and scrounge and toast subs, and remake the subs that I toasted badly, just to buy a car. A car that isn‚Äôt even 100 percent mine. My brother technically owns 40 percent of it and somehow manages to drive it 80 percent of the time.

But I don‚Äôt say any of this to Amber for fear she might fling a tray of cookies at me. Instead I watch as Madison pats Amber‚Äôs back. ‚ÄúYou need to breathe, okay?‚Äù she says. ‚ÄúEat a macaron. They have lavender in them, right? That‚Äôs supposed to be calming.‚Äù

‚ÄúLavender just makes me think of Iris, which makes me enraged,‚Äù Amber says.

We all look across the yard to where Iris is seated with Paige Breckner. Together, she and Paige hold the titles of class president three years running (Iris), most popular girl in our grade (Paige), and cutest couple in our school (collectively).

Though ‚Äúcutest‚Äù isn‚Äôt quite right. I don‚Äôt think anyone who knows her would use the word cute to describe anything relating to Iris Huang. Objectively, she has a roundness-of-face and smallness-of-stature that could traditionally be deemed cute. But she‚Äôs also ruthless and unforgiving and, some would say, ill-mannered and incredibly unpleasant. Somehow, this doesn‚Äôt seem to affect her political standing, but then again, that often seems to be the case in the real world as well.

But Paige and Iris have been the longest-enduring couple on record during our time at the Prospect-Landower School for Girls, and so they are automatically termed ‚Äúcutest,‚Äù because that‚Äôs shorter than ‚Äúlongest-enduring couple on record during our time at the Prospect-Landower School for Girls.‚Äù

I saw them once—I mean I‚Äôve seen them lots of times—but once, after school, I saw them sitting on the low wall outside the lit building, sharing a pair of earbuds and listening to something on one of their phones. Their heads were bent together, and then all of a sudden Paige started dancing in her spot, mouthing along the words to whatever song it was.

Iris looked up at her, smiled, and then looked back at the phone. Paige started bopping harder, lip-synching more emphatically, pointing at Iris.

Iris ducked her head, blushed, focused on the screen until Paige got to her feet, took Iris‚Äôs hands, and pulled her up, trying to get her to jump around. Iris looked flustered but ‚Ä¶ endeared, I guess. Fond in a way I had never seen her look.

When Iris finally relented and joined in dancing, the earbud jerked right out of her ear. She scrambled for it, accidentally yanking the other out of Paige‚Äôs ear. They both ended up bent over laughing, leaning on each other for support.

It was sweet—that‚Äôs why it stuck with me. A rare moment where Iris didn‚Äôt seem completely steely but instead kind of awkward and fumbly and smitten.

So maybe ‚Äúcutest‚Äù still means cutest, even where she‚Äôs involved.

Right now, Paige is chatting with Sudha Prabhu, laughing behind one hand as Sudha gestures animatedly, while Iris looks for all the world like she‚Äôs waiting in an airport terminal and her flight has just been canceled. In a sea of pink, she is unrelentingly purple.

At my table, Madison grins at Amber. ‚ÄúDeep breaths.‚Äù

‚ÄúI just want everything to be perfect,‚Äù Amber says, eyes wide and strangely earnest as she looks around the table at each of us. ‚ÄúIs it? Do you like the food? Are you having fun?‚Äù

We all affirm the quality of the food and the fun we‚Äôre having. I nod emphatically as I hork down a petit four.

‚ÄúI know it‚Äôs silly,‚Äù Amber says, ‚Äúbut it‚Äôs just, you know. Senior year and all. Everything we do is sort of the last time we get to do it. So it should be perfect, right?‚Äù

We double down on the reassurances, and finally Amber seems satisfied.

‚ÄúOkay. Okay, good.‚Äù She stands promptly, smooths down the front of her dress, takes a deep breath, and then heads off to the next table.

I adjust my own dress as the other girls start talking about school. I borrowed it from Zoe, so although it fits the color scheme, it‚Äôs also a little too short and a little too tight. When I came downstairs in it, my mom said ‚ÄúWowza,‚Äù and my dad, brow wrinkled, asked, ‚ÄúWhat kind of party is this again?‚Äù

Truth be told, I‚Äôd risk Amber‚Äôs wrath and wear the wrong color if it meant Zoe could be here with me, wearing this dress instead. She is my best friend, and there‚Äôs only so much a text can convey. Some of this stuff you just have to witness to fully appreciate. And most of it, I‚Äôd probably have a lot more fun witnessing with her.

But Zoe goes to Springdale High School, and I go to PLSG, and this isn‚Äôt really the kind of thing where you can bring a plus one. So I send her a quick text update and then listen in on Madison and Ainsley Stewart discussing some band they both watched on TV last night.

The rest of the luncheon goes well, much to Amber‚Äôs relief. We eat fancy finger foods. We toast each other with fizzy pink punch. There are speeches filled with assurances that this is going to be the ‚Äúbest year ever‚Äù and a shit ton of light applause.

Paige stands at one point and thanks Amber for hosting.

If you‚Äôre giving a presentation in class, Paige is the person who smiles at you when you catch her eye and nods encouragingly, like she‚Äôs actually listening. We had gym together freshman year, and whenever she was captain, she insisted on everyone counting off instead of picking teams.

When she finishes her toast, she turns to Iris with a smile. ‚ÄúDo you want to add something, babe?‚Äù

They share a look—in the silence, Paige‚Äôs expression shifts from hopeful to imploring—until finally Iris pushes her chair back and stands, holding up her glass of punch. She clears her throat.

‚ÄúCareless tourism and destructive fishing practices are destroying our world‚Äôs coral reefs,‚Äù she says, and then takes a drink.

I can‚Äôt tell if it‚Äôs a joke. Like, admittedly you probably shouldn‚Äôt joke about the destruction of our world‚Äôs coral reefs. A few people chuckle uncomfortably anyway.

Iris sits abruptly. Paige is still standing, her glass raised.

The look on her face is stricken, but somehow she manages to recover a smile. ‚ÄúThank you again, Amber,‚Äù she says. ‚ÄúThis is ‚Ä¶ a great way to end the summer.‚Äù

And that‚Äôs the last of the speeches. Conversation resumes around our little tables, and I excuse myself after a bit. Amber‚Äôs mom points me in the direction of the bathroom, but once I get inside the house, I realize that her directions of ‚Äúto the left and across from the music room‚Äù kind of hinge on knowing which room is the music room. Which I don‚Äôt.

So I head to the left and open the first door I come upon, and to my disappointment, it isn‚Äôt a bathroom but a bedroom.

I‚Äôm in luck though—there‚Äôs a bathroom en suite. I dash in and take care of business, and then I spend way too long sampling the products in pretty bottles on the bathroom counter.

I‚Äôm admiring the scents that I‚Äôve so expertly layered together (by squirting three random lotions on at the same time) when I hear sounds from the outer room: voices approaching and then the closing of the bedroom door. Sealing the voices inside.

‚Äú—believe you would act like that.‚Äù

‚ÄúI didn‚Äôt want to make a speech. I thought that was obvious.‚Äù

‚ÄúCoral reefs? Seriously?‚Äù

‚ÄúTell me we shouldn‚Äôt be more concerned about the state of the coral reefs.‚Äù

Paige and Iris.

I‚Äôd always thought they were a good pair. People don‚Äôt like Iris, generally, but they respect that she gets shit done. Conversely, everyone loves Paige. She‚Äôs friendly and kind, neutral good through and through. She softens Iris. And I guess Iris gives her an edge. What‚Äôs that saying—iron fist in a velvet glove? Iris is the former, and Paige is the latter.

‚ÄúI‚Äôm not saying it‚Äôs not true, I‚Äôm saying it‚Äôs not relevant to the situation. This isn‚Äôt a freaking Envirothon meeting!‚Äù

‚ÄúPlease. You know I don‚Äôt like how those Envirothon kids conduct themselves.‚Äù

‚ÄúYou couldn‚Äôt think of one nice thing. About Amber, or the summer, or school, or anything. One nice thing. You could‚Äôve said the punch is good.‚Äù

‚ÄúThe punch tastes like Windex.‚Äù

‚ÄúIris.‚Äù

‚ÄúWhat?‚Äù

‚ÄúAt the very least, would it have been so hard to put on a pink dress?‚Äù

‚ÄúI‚Äôm not gonna do something just because someone tells me to.‚Äù

‚ÄúYou do tons of things because someone tells you to! You wear shoes in restaurants! You obey seat belt laws!‚Äù

‚ÄúThere‚Äôs a big difference between doing something to prevent myself from flying through a car windshield and doing something to satisfy a meaningless color scheme at a meaningless party that neither of us actually care about.‚Äù

‚ÄúI care about it,‚Äù Paige says, and something in her voice sounds frayed. ‚ÄúBut that doesn‚Äôt matter to you. What I want. You never even ask me. You just assume. You always—always—just assume.‚Äù

Silence follows. And in this silence, I realize several things—first, that this is not just a little spat about a speech or a dress code. ‚ÄúI don‚Äôt want to do this,‚Äù Iris says finally.

A pause. ‚ÄúDo you understand though? About the dress? And the party? Do you get that it‚Äôs important to me? And, like, how something that matters to me should be important to you, too?‚Äù

‚ÄúIt‚Äôs stupid though,‚Äù Iris says. ‚ÄúThis whole thing is stupid. If it was something that actually mattered, I would—‚Äù She cuts off, starts again. ‚ÄúYou know I would‚Ä¶‚Äù She doesn‚Äôt finish.

‚ÄúYou would what?‚Äù A beat. ‚ÄúWhat would you do?‚Äù

‚ÄúI don‚Äôt know.‚Äù Iris sounds sullen. ‚ÄúI would act like I cared more.‚Äù

It‚Äôs quiet. Behind me, a bead of water drips from the faucet into the sink.

And then there are footsteps in the outer room. I can‚Äôt tell which of them has moved toward the other. Or if they‚Äôve moved away.

When Paige speaks again, her voice is thick. ‚ÄúI love you, Iris,‚Äù she says. ‚ÄúBut you‚Äôre the most selfish person I‚Äôve ever met.‚Äù

I press my ear to the door. I‚Äôm only human, after all, and this is possibly the best bit of drama I‚Äôve unwittingly stumbled onto in the whole of my high school career.

‚ÄúWhat are you saying?‚Äù

‚ÄúI love you,‚Äù she says again, and I‚Äôm fairly certain she‚Äôs crying now. ‚ÄúBut I want you to be different. I want, I wa—‚Äù Her voice hitches, like a sob. ‚ÄúI want you to be better than you are.‚Äù

I dated a boy named Will Sorenson for almost a whole semester in tenth grade. January to April. We were going to go to his junior prom together that year, but he broke up with me just two weeks shy.

We were in his basement, and he was playing an online role-playing game called Battle Quest. His character—a humanoid dragon named Alphoneus Centurion—was approaching a snow-covered vista with a monster in his sights when Will glanced over at me and said, ‚ÄúSo I don‚Äôt know, I just think maybe we should break up, you know?‚Äù

Like we had been having a conversation this whole time that I had somehow missed. I pressed him to explain as Alphoneus Centurion launched a series of attacks against the monster.

‚ÄúI just think that when you‚Äôre with someone, you should ‚Ä¶ feel something. Right?‚Äù

‚ÄúYou don‚Äôt feel anything with me?‚Äù

‚ÄúI feel regular with you,‚Äù he said. ‚ÄúBut I don‚Äôt feel ‚Ä¶ you know. Well, I mean, if you knew, then you‚Äôd understand, and you‚Äôd want me to feel that with someone else. Alphoneus raised his ax. The monster was a goner.

Up until this moment, I thought that was the most crushing thing you could say to someone you‚Äôre dumping. That you feel regular with them. It sort of managed to negate every sweet thing that we ever had together. Like it was all fake. One-sided, on my part. I was elated the first time he held my hand. I thought I might float off the sidewalk. And now looking back, I see that everything that was massive to me, everything that was meaningful—to him it was just regular.

But Paige Breckner just took the cake in the breakup department. I want you to be better than you are. If I were Iris, I would‚Äôve disintegrated on the spot.

Iris does no such thing. She just speaks, after a long pause, her voice in stark contrast to Paige‚Äôs. It‚Äôs calm. Crisp. No hint of tears.

‚ÄúAre you breaking up with me?‚Äù she says.

Paige doesn‚Äôt reply.

‚ÄúAre you. Breaking up with me?‚Äù Iris repeats, razor sharp.

‚ÄúYes,‚Äù Paige says.

I realize I‚Äôm holding my breath. Waiting for—something. A reaction. A movement. A sound. Anything.

And then a sound comes. A loud one in fact—the very clear and deliberate peal of a bell.

For a split second, it doesn‚Äôt make any sense. Paige breaks up with Iris and Iris responds by whipping out a handbell?

Then I realize. My phone.

I fumble with my bag. Purses are interdimensional sometimes, I swear— particularly when you‚Äôre trying to get something out of one. I finally extricate the phone, but three more texts follow in the intervening time—three more pealing bells—that only serve to further sink me.

The silence that follows is deafening.

I look into my palm. I might as well see the texts that are my undoing.

I need an update, Zoe‚Äôs first message reads.

Are you eating cahhhhviahhhr with Ahhhmber

And Mahhhhdison and Aaaaaainsley and Desk Lahhhmp

And all of your other fahhhhhbulous clahhhhssmates?

I almost laugh—it would be funny, normally, but now it‚Äôs so terrible it goes from terrible back into funny.

Until there‚Äôs the sound of footsteps that are most definitely approaching and a firm knock on the door directly in front of my face. Then it goes right back into terrible.

‚ÄúHello?‚Äù It‚Äôs Paige.

I hold my breath. Maybe if I‚Äôm perfectly still, perfectly silent, they‚Äôll think that they‚Äôd heard wrong.

‚ÄúWe heard your phone,‚Äù Paige says.

‚ÄúHow do you know it wasn‚Äôt your phone?‚Äù I reply. Because. I am. A moron.

I squeeze my eyes shut. Take a deep breath. And then I open the door.

It‚Äôs a Moment, between the three of us. There are just two hundred students at PLSG. Fifty girls a grade. And although I know Amber and Madison and Ainsley and Desk Lamp (there isn‚Äôt really a Desk Lamp, but Zoe likes to make fun of the names, how ‚Äúeveryone sounds like an item from a furniture catalog, seriously‚Äù) and all the others in a cursory manner, there aren‚Äôt many of them that I have had genuine Moments with.

The thing about Moments is that just because a moment is one, doesn‚Äôt mean it‚Äôs a good one. They are not all Special, or Cherished, as picture frames and embellished scrapbook inserts would have you believe.

This is more of the Painfully Awkward variety. Me, clutching my phone in the bathroom doorway, looking quite like my ear had just been pressed against the door—because my ear had just been pressed against the door. Paige, her face red, cheeks wet, eyes puffy. And Iris Huang, resplendent and terrifying in lavender YSL, looking at me with a quiet, smoldering, single-minded rage.

Paige speaks first. ‚ÄúWe didn‚Äôt know anyone was in here,‚Äù she says, and she‚Äôs clearly putting effort into sounding something close to normal, though she doesn‚Äôt bother to wipe the tears tracking down her cheeks.

‚ÄúI didn‚Äôt know anyone was out here. I didn‚Äôt hear anything,‚Äù I say, even though it‚Äôs a lie compounded by another lie.

The silence is unbearable.

‚ÄúI had the faucet on,‚Äù I say. ‚ÄúReally loud. And I pee pretty loud. I‚Äôm surprised you guys didn‚Äôt hear me, it was like Niagara Falls in here. Just really ‚Ä¶ very loud in volume. A lot of ‚Ä¶ liquids ‚Ä¶ flowing in a ‚Ä¶ noisy fashion.‚Äù

‚ÄúI‚Äôll just ‚Ä¶ leave you guys to it.‚Ä¶ Not that I know what it is, not that I heard anything,‚Äù I say, and make to leave, but Paige moves to the door first.

‚ÄúNo, I‚Äôm going,‚Äù she says, and then quickly walks out.

Iris crosses immediately to me and holds a finger up to my face. She forces me backward, back into the bathroom, where I stumble over the rug and catch myself on the fancy towel rack.

‚ÄúWhat did you hear?‚Äù she says.

‚ÄúNothing. I heard nothing.‚Äù

‚ÄúYou‚Äôre lying.‚Äù

‚ÄúI‚Äôm not.‚Äù

‚ÄúYou are, you‚Äôre terrible at it. If you go back out there and tell everyone— if you tell them—‚Äù She falters. And I would hardly believe it unless I saw it myself, but Iris‚Äôs eyes swiftly fill with tears. Her lower lip quivers. It legitimately quivers.

Her voice is thick when she speaks: ‚ÄúI will ruin you.‚Äù

And then she turns and leaves.

two

I go to work at Pinky‚Äôs on Sunday. The day after Iris declared that she would ruin me.

When I rejoined the party yesterday, she was nowhere in sight. Paige had resumed her seat, but she was looking decidedly worse for wear and the rest of her table had clearly noticed. Sudha had one arm around her, their heads bent together in conversation, and Alicia Smith was kneeling at her side, holding up a pink patterned napkin so Paige could wipe her eyes.

Word would travel fast. I didn‚Äôt need to tell anyone what I had heard. Furthermore, I didn‚Äôt want to tell anyone. First, because it was no one‚Äôs business, and second, because I didn‚Äôt want the wrath of Iris Huang to befall me and my family.

I replay it in my mind as I pull a sub out of the toaster and deposit it on the counter in front of me (a little charred around the edges, but hopefully that would escape notice). No one had ever sworn my ruin before.

I would just steer clear of them both at school. I‚Äôve managed to fly under the radar pretty effectively these last three years. It won‚Äôt be hard to pull off a fourth.

Stealth mode, I think as I cut and wrap up the sandwich in patterned Pinky‚Äôs paper.

Pinky‚Äôs is ‚Äúhome of the nine-inch sub.‚Äù Fifty percent more than a Subway sub! the sign declares.

‚ÄúI don‚Äôt think that‚Äôs right,‚Äù I said to my manager Aaron on my first day. ‚ÄúBecause Subway subs are twelve inches? So really it‚Äôs only seventy-five percent of a Subway sub?‚Äù

‚ÄúThey mean the six-inch,‚Äù he replied.

‚ÄúSo maybe they should say that?‚Äù

‚ÄúHome of the nine-inch sub, fifty percent more than a six-inch Subway sub,‚Äù Aaron contemplated, and then shook his head. ‚ÄúThat‚Äôs too much numerical information. It‚Äôs too cerebral.‚Äù

This was possibly the first time anyone had used the phrase ‚Äútoo cerebral‚Äù when describing Pinky‚Äôs advertising. Because someone somewhere in the Pinky‚Äôs marketing scheme had made the brilliant connection that sub sandwiches are vaguely phallic. And from that, all the penis-related Pinky sub campaigns were born.

Like the commercial where you see the guy standing from the back, and then a woman in front of him, and she says, ‚ÄúNine inches????‚Äù in this insane lusty voice, and then they pan to the side and show that he‚Äôs holding a Pinky sub right at groin height? It‚Äôs the worst. It is literally the worst. I‚Äôm a cog in the world‚Äôs dumbest corporate sandwich machine.

But I needed a job. And Pinky‚Äôs was hiring. So here I am.

Zoe comes over that evening when I get off work, and we ride our bikes to the Tropical Moose to get snow cones. The car is free—Alex is actually home for once—but I‚Äôm stiff from standing all day and I smell like Pinky‚Äôs. I want to ride, to feel the late summer air rushing through my hair, to pedal fast and feel the burn of it in my legs.

I get a pi√±a colada–flavored snow cone, Zoe gets a bubble gum one, and we manage to secure a table out front, the kind with the chairs that leave waffle patterns on the backs of your thighs.

We‚Äôre settled in when two little kids pass by clutching blue cones. A woman follows close behind but veers toward our table as they pass.

‚ÄúYou have beautiful hair,‚Äù she says to Zoe, who smiles and nods in reply, her mouth full of slush. She pulls a face at me when the woman has passed.

Zoe does have beautiful hair. The rest of her is pretty magnificent as well —dark eyes with the longest lashes, flawless brown skin. One time a barista at Starbucks wrote your smile is gorgeous on Zoe‚Äôs coffee cup. I took a picture of her holding the cup, smiling wide, and we put it on Tumblr, and to date it has over fifty thousand notes.

It makes sense to me, knowing Zoe as I do. Her outside matches her insides. I know goodness doesn‚Äôt manifest itself as beauty—she‚Äôs not pretty because she‚Äôs smart, or funny, or kind—but it‚Äôs fitting. She‚Äôs my favorite person, and if one of us had to grow up ridiculously beautiful, I‚Äôm glad it‚Äôs her. It just makes sense.

‚ÄúI have calc and lit with Kris,‚Äù Zoe says, continuing our previous conversation. ‚ÄúAnd AP Bio with Gabby, so that‚Äôs something. What‚Äôs your schedule like?‚Äù

I shrug. ‚ÄúWhat it‚Äôs always like.‚Äù

‚ÄúHey, how was the party? You never texted back.‚Äù

Now there is no way Iris Huang could be privy to this conversation. Zoe doesn‚Äôt even go to our school—she‚Äôs at Springdale, the public school in our generic Chicago suburb. We live in one of the neighborhoods that‚Äôs fare-and-a-half to get to if you take a cab from the city. PLSG is twenty minutes from Springdale, and most of the richest girls live even farther out. So the likelihood of Iris popping up and hearing me tell Zoe that I witnessed the Breakup of the Century (or at least of the Year to Come) is pretty slim. And usually I tell Zoe everything. But. Something in me just ‚Ä¶ doesn‚Äôt want to risk it.

So I just shrug again and say, ‚ÄúThe food was good. There was a lot of talk of this year being our year.‚Äù

‚ÄúWho did all the other years belong to?‚Äù

‚ÄúRight? I asked Madison that. She looked at me like I was crazy.‚Äù

‚ÄúToo bad she can‚Äôt buy a sense of humor.‚Äù

‚ÄúI‚Äôd sell her mine.‚Äù

‚ÄúYou could buy another car with the money.‚Äù

‚ÄúDo you think I‚Äôm funny enough for car money?‚Äù

‚ÄúFor sure. You‚Äôre minimum pre-owned Toyota Corolla funny.‚Äù

I grin.

It‚Äôs quiet as we finish our snow cones, but not a bad kind of quiet. With some people I feel the need to fill the space, but with Zoe, it‚Äôs okay. It doesn‚Äôt have to be constant. We can listen to the hum of crickets, the buzz of the tree frogs.

We ride back to my place when we‚Äôre finished. I ask Zoe if she wants to come in, but she shakes her head.

‚ÄúI should get back. It‚Äôs officially a school night.‚Äù

‚ÄúUgh.‚Äù

‚ÄúIt was good while it lasted.‚Äù Zoe smiles—her radiant Starbucks cup smile —and all at once I‚Äôm struck with something Amber said at the party—senior year and all. How everything we do is sort of the last time we get to do it. Tomorrow is the very last Back to School for us. Back to high school, anyway. Meaning that today—tonight, right now—is the end of the very last summer of the way things are. Next summer we might go off to college at different times. To different cities. Different time zones. I can‚Äôt imagine it.

Or rather, I can. I just don‚Äôt want to.

I don‚Äôt say any of that. ‚ÄúText me tomorrow,‚Äù I say instead.

‚ÄúWill do.‚Äù Zoe gets back on her bike. ‚ÄúNight, Claude.‚Äù

‚ÄúNight.‚Äù

I watch her pedal away.

three

My mom makes me stand on the front steps before I leave for school the next morning so she can take a picture. The annual first day of school stoop photo. Frames hang in the kitchen for each of us—a big print scroll across the top declaring My School Days with twelve little spaces underneath for pictures.

‚ÄúExcuse me, I am one photographer.‚Äù

Brilliant.

Zoe and I volunteer at Roosevelt-Hart on Thursday evenings.

A Midsummer Night‚Äôs Dream is really an ensemble show. There‚Äôs no singular main character or narrator but a few different groups of characters, all of equal importance.

Next there are the Mechanicals—a rough-and-tumble group of people tasked with putting on a play for the Duke‚Äôs impending wedding. And last there are the Fairies—the King, Oberon, and the Queen, Titania, and their respective crews.

Oberon and Titania are fighting, and the world is all out of whack because of it. Meanwhile, Hermia and Lysander run away to get married when Hermia‚Äôs dad says she has to marry Demetrius. forty-one

* * *

I set my computer aside and lie on my bed, and I can‚Äôt help but think of the first day of preschool.

I knew that school was a place that Julia went off to every day. Alex had started going, too, and I knew that he liked it—the coloring and the block corner and the little round tokens you could exchange for treats on special days.

But I wanted to stay home with my mom, who was still just part-time back then, working evenings after my dad got home from work.

On the first day of preschool, my mom held my hand and walked me into the classroom. She got me situated at a table where a couple of kids were doing puzzles and then hung back a bit while I started in on mine. The puzzle was tricky, but I was getting the hang of it—I almost had it—

Then I turned around and saw that she wasn‚Äôt there anymore.

I cried and cried and cried when I realized she had left. I cried even as a little girl from one of the other tables came up to me. She was wearing denim shorts and a purple striped T-shirt. Her hair was arranged in little twists, clasped at the ends by plastic flower barrettes in an array of neon colors. The barrettes clicked against one another as she moved toward me.

‚ÄúShe‚Äôs gonna come back,‚Äù she said as I hiccupped in between sobs. ‚ÄúYour mama. She‚Äôll come back later. Mrs. Parson said.‚Äù

Her eyes were very solemn. I didn‚Äôt understand solemnity or concern; I couldn‚Äôt define the fact that she was looking at me like I was important. Like I mattered. But it stopped my crying, momentarily.

I didn‚Äôt know what to say. So I reached out and touched one of her barrettes.

I admired it, the perfect flower shape, and then held it out to her.

‚ÄúYou can keep it,‚Äù Zoe said. And just like that, we were friends.

I sit up.

And stand and cross over to my dresser, to the jewelry box on top. I slide open one of the tiny drawers, top left. If the jewelry box had a heart, this would be it.

I pick up the barrette. Clasp it hard and hold my fist against my mouth. I know that it will press a daisy shape into my palm, and I don‚Äôt even care.

I cry hard.

forty-nine

I‚Äôm crossing through the Grove on Thursday before the start of dress rehearsal. The leaves on the trees have thinned—most have fallen in a carpet of yellows and reds and browns—and I see the figures up ahead before I hear them.

He grins full-out now, and then we kiss.

Just once, soft and light. His lips part just the slightest bit, and when we break apart, he lets out a breath. I think for a second he‚Äôs going to say something, but then he brings one hand to my waist and kisses me again, fuller, deeper, longer. Awesome sells it short. Any positive adjective I can possibly think of is not good enough, not potent enough, not worthy to describe it. It‚Äôs just ‚Ä¶ something else.

When we finally separate again, I rest my forehead against Gideon‚Äôs.

‚ÄúYou know what?‚Äù

‚ÄúHm?‚Äù He sounds a little dazed.

‚ÄúThat had everything on my must-have list,‚Äù I say, and then drop down to a whisper: ‚ÄúLuxury. Affordable. Industry standard.‚Äù

Gideon lets out an unholy snort, and I dissolve into giggles. I bury my face in the crook of his neck, and we both laugh, holding each other tight.

fifty-seven

‚ÄúThanks for coming to the show,‚Äù I say. It‚Äôs late, and Zoe and I are in bed. My back is resting against the headboard, my knees pulled up to my chest. I feel wide-awake, like I just got a hundred hours‚Äô sleep.

‚ÄúI wouldn‚Äôt have missed it. A-plus costumes. Stupendous. And the show was good, too, I guess.‚Äù She cuddles Mr. English closer and then looks up at me. ‚ÄúBut before we get into the whos and whats of the fancy school theater scene, I need to know what it was like to kiss the space prince. I need ‚Ä¶ a full-length analysis.‚Äù

I consider it for a moment. I could consider it for a lot of moments, if left unchecked. Not just the kiss in the hall outside the auditorium, but the ones in the parking lot afterward and at the cast party and on the street outside after the cast party. Gideon with his cheeks flushed and his eyes bright, close enough that he was blurry, saying I like you, you know. Like so much, so much, so much—each one punctuated by a kiss to a different place.

‚ÄúLike going into an instance,‚Äù I say. ‚ÄúLike ‚Ä¶ existing apart from everything else.‚Äù

Zoe just looks at me for a second and then gets out of bed and begins circling the room.

‚ÄúWhat?‚Äù

‚ÄúThat was so sappy, I have to walk it off.‚Äù

‚ÄúIt‚Äôs not sappy, you asked me to—you said I should—‚Äù

She‚Äôs hopping around now, flapping her hands. ‚ÄúWalking it off.‚Äù

I throw an arm over my eyes and laugh. ‚ÄúSorry. Yeah. It‚Äôs silly. But—‚Äù

‚ÄúIt‚Äôs also sweet,‚Äù she says, crawling back on the bed. ‚ÄúReally sweet, Claude.‚Äù

It‚Äôs quiet for a bit, both of us just thinking. Until I finally turn back to Zoe and say, ‚ÄúI have to tell you something.‚Äù

‚ÄúOh shit. Are you and Gideon running away into the woods to get married? Because I just saw a play about the dangers of that.‚Äù

I grin. ‚ÄúNo.‚Äù

‚ÄúWhat is it then?‚Äù

‚ÄúI really like TION. Like, I kind of love them.‚Äù

Zoe smiles. ‚ÄúI sort of got that impression.‚Äù

‚ÄúLike, not even a little bit ironically. I genuinely love them. If one of them needed a kidney and I was a match, I would genuinely give them my kidney.‚Äù

‚ÄúWould you give me a kidney?‚Äù

‚ÄúI‚Äôd give you both kidneys.‚Äù

She wraps an arm around me. Rests her head against mine for a moment.

Tomorrow, we‚Äôre going to see Julia and Mark, and I‚Äôll meet Jack for the first time. The nurse will tell us that the tallest oaks were once the smallest acorns, and Julia will smile at her but make a face at me when the nurse leaves. My son the acorn, she‚Äôll say, and laugh and wipe her eyes.

The show will go on again that night and be every bit as wonderful. And I‚Äôll call Gideon when it‚Äôs over and talk to him long after everyone has fallen asleep, the phone growing warm against my ear. As we say good night, I will want to say I love you. I won‚Äôt say it. But just the thought of it will keep me awake long after. The best kind of awake, the purest caffeine pumping through your veins, where you never want to stop feeling what you‚Äôre feeling, can‚Äôt bear the thought of interrupting it, even with sleep.

But right now, it‚Äôs me and Zoe.

‚ÄúOkay,‚Äù she says. ‚ÄúI know you have pictures of TION somewhere. Walk me through it. Give me a crash course.‚Äù

I smile, reaching for my phone. ‚ÄúLet‚Äôs start from the beginning.‚Äù
